OF THE

AUTHOR OF ROBINSON CRUSOE.

DANIEL Daron, the author of numerous works of fiction, among
which the " History of the Plague " has attained a certain popularity,
and " Robinson Crusoe " a lasting and world-wide renown, was born
in the parish of St. Giles, Cri plegate, in the city of London, in the
year 1661. He was educal at a dissent' school at Newington
Green; his father, astrict dissenter, intending rim for the priesthood.
But this project seems to have been abandoned, for some unknown
season, and young Daniel became a tradesmen, like his father. Some--
thinn of the roving, restless nature, so admirabl described in
" Rdhinson Crusoe," seems to have been inherent in fee himself;
for his life is full of strange vicissitudes, and he a pears continually
involved in trouble on one account or another. Viie find him taking
part in the most ill-considered and unfertunate enterprise of his
time, the Rebellion of Monmouth, and narrowly escaping what the
cynical spirit of the time dubbed " a merry o-round at rope fair,"
namely, death by strangulation, for the 0 once. In after-life he
was hotly embroiled in the political quarrels of his age. At one
time he was compelled to stand in the pillory, as a punishment for
writinn a well-timed and thoughtful pamphlet on "The shortest wa
with the Dissentcrs." The )opulace, sympathizin with and -
miring him, crowned the pil cry with flowers, an converted his
penance into a triumph; but this could not relieve him from the

urdcn of the heavy fine he was compelled to pay, and which ham-
pered him for years afterwards. At another time he suffered along
unprisoumeul, obtaining his release after twb years by the interven-
tion of Harley, Earl of Oxford. But he had not the art of advancing
his fortunes, though his evident merit procured him em loyment in
several matters of eonse nence; and, at the end of his ll) life, he
was in poverty and new eet. He died in his native paris of St.
Giles, Cripplegate, Apri 24, 1781, at the age of seventy, and was
interred in Bunhill Fields burial-ground. -

The political works of Defoe are numerous, and had a sensible
effect on the times in which he lived. A thorou h Eashshman, out-
spoken, vehement, aud uncompromisi , he s 10" out lustily
against abuses and wrong-doing wherever to found them; and in many
rcsliects his ideas were greatly in advance of his time. Some of his
schemes may have been Visionary and impracticable; but it is pleasant
to hear his sturdy voice raised, and to see his nervous pen wielded so
unliinchingly in advocacy of his nrineiples, and to mark hownnvaryingly
those principles point to moderation, melcy and the low of kindness.
His Review, which he conducted for nine years, may certainly be
considered as the pioneer of the Spec/aim, il'al/er, Guardian, and
other collections of essays which enriched the. literature of the first
half of the eighteenth century.

llis political writings, however, have mostly passed away with the
truublous times which gave them birth, and to which their interest
was confined. It was as a writer of fiction that Defoe was to
achieve more lasting fame, Two, at. least, of his works have a value
and a significance iiite irrespective of time and place,--the one as a
record of a national calamity, the other as a wonderful piece of ima-
'inary autobiography. These two books are the "History of the
fungi": of 1665," and the " Adventures of Robinson Crusoe."

.-\t the time when the scourge was deciuiatinw London, Daniel
Defoe was only four years old. It could not, ticrel'ore, be from
memory that be so vividly described its incidents; though, doubtless,
in his youth he heard man)v an anecdote about the pestilence and its
ravages, from men of mat urcr years, on whose inenioricsit must have
made an indelible impression. But his chief talent lay in the
management. of detail and accurate description; and it is ditlicult to
imagine, as we read the graphic record of the state of the terror-
strickcn, desolate city, that we have not before us the real daily
notes of at.- actual indwellcr in the city of the laguc. In Robinson
Crusoe the. Same marvellous ower is shown, nit in a much hiuher
degree. With matehless 5k" 1, the doubts and sorrows, the shifts
and cxpedients, the little domestic triumphs and disappointments of
the ship-wrecked mariner in his solitart' home are put before the
reader in the very form most- ealculaled to enlist his interest and
sympathies. So thoroughly has the author identified himself with the
hereof his romance, that Defoe disappears, and it. is Robinson Crusoe
himself who becomes a living personality. and moves and speaks
before the reader, and becomes as clearly and distinctly personified
as any hero of real life.

The first edition of Robinson Crusoe was published in 1719. In
the century and a half that has since elapsed, hundreds of edi-
tions have aipcared in all European languages. Everywhere the
book has \rorlted its way; and as for the German version, " Robinson
dc. J angel-e" is chronicled as one of the greatest succches ot' the
eminent llanibttrgh lit-tn of Cuinpe and Co.

In the present volume, the original text, slightly shortened, has
been adhered to. The operation of modernizing the language, ne-
cessary, perhaps, in a scientific work, would have taken away one of
the chief charms from the narrative of the simple sailor, who is
therefore left to tell his tale in his own rough, pathetic, old-fashioned
way. The drawings are from an eminent German pencil, and apart
fil'lmn their artistic value, serve the purpose of thoroughly illustrating
t e text.
ROBINSON'S FAMILY, Era--ms ELOPEMENI FROM HIS PARENTS.

I WAS born in the year 1632, in the cit of York, of a good family,
though not of that country, my father ein a foreigner of Bremen,
named Kreutznaer, who settled first at Hul . He got a ood estate
by merchandise, and leavin of his trade, lived afterwar at York '
from whence he had marrie my mother, whose relations were named
Robinson, a very good family in that country, and after whom I was
so called, that is to say, Robinson Krentznaer; but, by the usual
corru tion of words in England, we are now called, nay, we call
ourse ves, and write our name, Crusoe; and so my companions always
called me.

I had two elder brothers, one of whom was lieutenant-colonel to an
English regiment of foot in Flanders, formerly commanded by the
famous Colonel Lockhart, and was killed at the battle near Dunkirk
against the Spaniards. What became of my second brother, I never
knew, any more than my father and mot er did know what was
become of me.

Being the third son of the family, and not bred to any trade, my
head began to be filled very early with rambling thoughts. My
father, who was very aged, had given me a competent share of
loami ,as far as house education andacountry free school nerall
go, an designed me for the law; but I would be satis ed wit
nothinv but going to sea; and my inclination to this led me so
ntrougl'y against the will, na , the commands of my father, and
against all the entreaties an persuasions of my mother and other
friends, that there seemed to be something fatal in that pro nsion of
nature, tending directly to the life of misery which was to efall me:

1 B
My father, a wise and grave man, gave me serious and excellent
counsel against what he foresaw was my design. He called me one
mornin into his chamber, where he was confined by the. gout, and
tcxpostn atcd very warmly with me upon this subject; he asked me
what reasons, more than a mere wandering inclination, I had for
leaving his house, and my native country, where I might be well
introduced, and had a prospect of raisinir my fortune, by application
and industry, with a life of case and casurei He told me it was
men of desperate fortunes, on one hand: or of superior fortunes, on
the other, who went abroad upon adventures, aspiring to rise by
enterprise, and make themselves famous in undertakings of nature
out of the common road; that these things were all either too far
above me. or too far below me ; that mine was the middle state, or
what might be called the upper station of low life, which he had
found, by long experience, was the best state in the world, the most
suited to human ha )piness; not armed to the miseries and hard-
shi s, the labour an sufferings, of the mechanic part of mankind,
an not embarrassed with the pride, luxury, ambition, and envy of
the upper part of mankind : he told me, I might judge of the happi-
ness of this state by one thing, \'iz., that this was the state of hle
which all other pcoiilc envied ; that kings have frequently lamented
the miserable consequences of bein" born to great things, and wished
they had been placed in the middle of two extremes, between the
mean and the rreat; that the wise man gave his testimony to this
as the just stan ardol' true felicity, when he prayed to have "neither
povert nor riches."

He adc me observe it, and I should alwnrs find, that the calami.
ties of life were shared among the upper and lower part of mankind;
but that the' middle station had the fewest disasters, and was not
thoSed to so many vicissitudes as the higher or lower part of man-
kind; nay, they were not subjected to so many distempers and
uneasinesses, either of body or mind, as those were, who, bv vicious
living, luxury, and extravagancies, on one hand, or, by hard labour,
want of necessaries, and mean and insullicientdiet, on the other hand,
bring distem )CI'S upon themselves by the natural consequences of
their way of 'ving; that the middle station of life was calculated for
all kind of virtues, and all kind of cujoyments; that peace and
plenty were the handmaids of a middle fortune; that temperance,
moderation, quietness, health, societv, all agreeable diversions, and
all desirable pleasures were the blessings attending the middle
station of tile. ; that this way men went- sileutly and smoothly throu h
the world, and comfortably out of it, not embarrassed with t e
labours ol' the bands or of the head, not sold to the life of slavery
for dail bread, or harassed with perplexed circumstances, which rob
the son of peace, and the body of rest; not enraged with the passion
of envy, or secret burning lust of ambition for great things; but, in
easy circumstances, sliding gently through the world, and sensibly"venous or momson mason. a

tasting the sweets of living. without the bitter; feelin that they are
happy, and learning by every day's experience, to now it more
semi )ly.

After this he resscd me earnestly, and in the most afl'ectionate
manner, not to p ay the young man, nor to precipitate myself into
miseries which nature, and the station of life I was born in seemed
to have rovided against; that I was under no necessity of seeking
my brea ; that he would do well for me, and endeavour to enter me
fairly into the station of life which he had been just recommending
to me; and that if I was not very easy and ha py in the world, it.
must be my mere fate, or fault, that must hin or it; and that he
should have nothing to answer for, having thus discharged his duty
in \rarniiw me against measures which he knew would be to my hurt;
in a wow , that he would do very kind thin for me if I would
stay :ul settle at home as he directed; he to (1 me I had in elder
brother for an example, who had run inro the army, where e was
killed during the Low Country wars; that if I did take this foolish
step, God would not bless me; and I would have leisure, hereafter,
to reflect upon having neglected his counsel, when there might be
none to assist in my reeove .

I observed the tears run rdown his face very plentifully, especially
when he spoke of my brother who was killed , and that, when he
spoke of my having leisure to repent, and none to assist me, he was
so moved, that he broke off the discourse, and told me his heart was
so full, he could say no more to me.

I was sincerely affected with this discourse; as, indeed, who
could be otherwise? and I resolved not to think of going abroad any
more, but to settle at home, according to my father's desire. But
alas! a few days were it all oli': and, in short, to prevent an of
my father's further importunities, in a few weeks after-I reso red
to run quite away from him. However,l did not act so hastily,
neither, as my first heat of resolution prompted; but I took my
mother, at a time when I thought heralittle pleasanter than ordinary,
and told her that my thoughts were so entirely bent upon seeing
the world, that I should never settle to anythinw with resolution
enough to go through with it, and my father had better give me his
consent than force me to go without it; that I was now e' htecn
years old, which was too late to go apprentice to a trade, or c erk to
an attorney; that Iwas sure, i I did, would never serve out
my time, and I should certainly run away from my master before my
time was out, and go to sea; and if she would speak to my father to
let me make but one voyage abroad, if I came home again, and did
not like it, I would go no more; and 1 would promise by a double
diligence, to recover the time I had lost.

This put my mother in a great passion: she told me she knew it
would he to no purpose to speak to my father upon any such a sub-
ject; that he knew too well what was my interest to give his consent

324- ADVZNTUEES 01' 30313501! 0111180].

to anything so much- for my hurt; and that she wondered how I
could think of any such thing, after such a discourse as I had from
my father, and such kind and tender ex rcssions as she knew my
fniher had used: that for her art, she wo d not have so much hand in
my destruction; and I 3110 d never have it to say, that my mother
was willing when my father was not.

Though my mother refused to move it to my father, yet, as 1 have
heard atterwards, she reported all the discourse to him; and that
my father, after showing a great concern at it, said to her With a sigh :
" That. boy mioht be happy if he would stay at home; but if he goes
abroad, he \viil be the most miserable wretoh that ever was born ;
I cannot give consent to it."

 

It was not till almost a year after this that I broke loose; though
in the meantime I continued obstinately deaf to all proposals of
settling to business, and frequently expostulating wit my father
and mother about their being so positively determined against what
they knew my inclinations rompted me to. But being one day at
Hull, whether I wont casually, and without any purpose of making
an elopement at that time, and one of my companions then goin
to London by sea in his father's ship, and prompting me to go wit 1
then by the common allurement of seafaring men, viz., that it should
ecst me nothing for my passage, 1 consulted neither father nor
mother any more, nor so much as sent them word of it; but left
them to hear of it as they might, without asking God's blessing,
or my father's, without any consideration of circumstances or conse-
quences, and in an ill hour, God knows. .
FIRST ADVENTURES AT SEA, AND EXPERIENCE OF A mum LIFE.
VOYAGE 'IO GUINEA.

ON the lst September, 1651, I went on board a ship bound for
London. Never any yo adventurer's misfortunes, I believe,
began sooner, or continued anger, than mine. The ship had no
sooner got. out of the Humber, than the wind began to blow, and the
waves to rise, in :1 most frightful manner; and as I had never been
at sea before, I was most inexyressibly sick in body, and terrified in
mind; I be n now seriously to reflect upon what I had done, and
how justly Fives overtaken lzlthe judgment of Heaven, for wickedly
leaving my father's house. I the good counsels of my arents, my
father's tears, and my mother's entreaties, came now resh to my
mind; and m conscience, which was not yet come to the itch of
hardness to which it has been since, re'proaehed me with t e 0011-
tempt of advice, and the abandonment o my duty.

All this while the storm increased, and the sea, which I had
never been upon before, went very high, though nothing like what
I have seen many times since; no, nor what I saw a few days after;
but, such as it was, enough to shoot me then, who was but a yonn
sailor, and had never known anything of the matter. I expecte
every wave would have swallowed us up, and that eve time the
ship fell down, as I thought, in the trough or hollow oft 6 see, we
should never rise more; and in this agony of mind I made nmfilfy
vows and resolutions, that if it would please God to s are .m ' e
this voyage, if ever I got my foot once on dry land, we d
directly home to my father, and never set it into a ship again whigl:

10
I lived; that I would take his advice, and never run myself into
such miseries as these any more. Now I saw plainly the goodness
of his observations about the middle station of life; how easy, how
comfortable, he had lived all his days, and never had been exposed
to tcmpcsls at sea or troubles on shore; and I resolved that I would,
like a true repenting rodigal, go home to m father.

These wise and so or thoughts continue during the storm, and
indeed sometime after; but the next day as the wind was abated,
and the sea. calmer, I begun to be a little inurcd to it. However, I
was very grave that day. being also a little sea-sick still; but towards
night the weather cleared up, the wind was quite over, and a charm.
ing' fine evening followed: the sun went down perfectly clear, and.
rose so the next morning; and havinglittlc or no wind, and a smooth
sea, the sun shining upon it, the sight was, as I thought, the most
delightful that I ever saw.

I had slept well in the night, and was now no more sea-sick, but
verv cheerful, looking with wonder upon the sea that was so rough
and terrible the day before, and could be so calm and pleasant in a
little time after.

 

And now, lest my good resolutions should continue, my companion,
who had indeed enticed me away, came to me, and said, " Well, Bob,"
clapping me on the shoulder, "how do you do after it? I warrant
you you were frightened, wa'n't you, last night, when it blew a cap-
l'ull of wind ?"--" A cap-full, do you call it i?" said I; " 'twas a terrible
storm."--" A storm you fool P" replies he, " do you call that a storm!
Why, it was nothing at. all ; give us but a good ship, and sea-room,
and we think nothing of such a squall of wind as that; you are but
a. fresh water sailor, Bob; come, let us nmke a bowl of punch, and
we'll forget all that. D'ye see what charming weather 'tis now 'r'"
To make short this sad part of my story, we went the way of all
sailors; the punch was made, and I was made drunk with it; and in
one night's wickedness I drowned all my repentance, all my reflec-
tions upon my past conduct, and all my resolutions for the future.
1n a word, as the sea was returned to its smoothiess of surface and

v
settled calmness by the abatement of the storm, so the hurry of
my tho hts being over, my fears and apprehensions of being
swallowe up b t e sea forgotten, and the current of my former
desires returns , I entirely orgot the vows and promises I had
made in in distress.

The sixt day of our being at sea we came into Yarmouth Roads '
the wind having been contrary and the weather calm, we had
made but little way since the storm. Here we were obliged to
come to an anchor, and here we lay, the wind continuing contrary,
viz. at south-west, for seven or eight days, during which time a great
many ships from Newcastle came into the same roads, as a common
harbour where the ships might wait for a wind for the river Thames.
We had not, however, rid here so long, but we should have tided up
the river, but that the wind blew too fresh; and, after we had lain
four or five (is s, blew very hard. However, the roads being reckoned
as good as a arbour, the anchorage d, and our ground tackle
very strouo, our men were unconcerne , and not in the least appre-
hensive of!3 danger, but spent the time in rest and mirth, after the
manner of the sea. But the eighth day, in the morning, the wind
increased, and we had all hands at work to strike our topmasts, and
make everything snug and close, that the ship might ride as easy as

ossible. By noon the sea went very high indeed, and our ship rode
orecastle in, shipped several seas, and we thought, once or twice our
anchor had come ome; upon which our master ordered out the sheet
anchor; so that we rode with two anchors ahead, and cables veered
out to the better end. '

By this time it blew a terrible storm indeed; and now I began to
see terror and amazement in the faces of even the seamen themselves.
The master was vigilant in the business of preserving the ship; but.
as he went in and out of his cabin by me, I could hear him softly
it]? to himself several times, " Lord, be merciful to us! we shall be

lost; we shall be all undone -," and the like.

The storm was so violent, that I saw what is not often seen, the
master, the boatswain, and some others, more sensible than the rest,
at their prayers, and ex ccting every moment the ship would go to

As my new patron, or master, had taken me home to his house,

and when he came home again from his cruise, he ordered me to lie
in the cabin, to look after the ship.

Here I meditated nothing but my escape, and what method I

my clothes, for the weather was hot to extremity, and took the
water: but when I came to the ship, my difficulty was still greater
to know how to get on board: for as she lay around, and high out
of the water, there was nothing in my reach to ay hold of. I swam
round her twice, and the second time I spied a small piece of rope,
which I wondered I did not see at first, hang down by the fore-
ehaius so low. as that with great dillieulty I got hold of it, and b
the help of that rope got into the forecastle of the ship. llcre
found that the ship was bilged, and had a great deal of water in her
hold ; but that she la}r so on the side of a bank of hard sand, or rather
earth, that her stern lay lifted up upon the bank, and her head low,
almost to the water. By this means all her uarter was free, and
all that was in that part was dry ; for you may c sure my first work

first, I found that all the ship's provisions were dry and untouched
oy the water; and being very well disposed to eat, I went to the

had indeed need enough of, to spirit me for what was before me.
New I wanted nothing but a boat, to furnish myself with many
things which I foresaw would be very necessary to me.

It was in vain to sit still and wish for what was not to be had,
and this extremity roused my application: we had several spare
yards, and two or three lar e spars of wood, and a spare topmast or
two in the ship; I resolve to fall to work with these, and flung as
many overboard as I could manage for their weight, tying every one
with a rape, that they might not drive away. When this was done,
1 went down the ship's side, and pulling them to me, I tied four of
them fast together at both ends, as well as I could, in the form of a
raft, and laying two or three short pieces of plank upon them, cross-
ways, I found I could walk upon it. very well, but that it was not
able to bear any rcat weight, the pieces being too light: so I went
to work, and wit. the carpenter's saw I cut a. spare to mast into
three lengths, and added them to my raft, with a wreat de of labour
and pains. But the hope of furnishing myself with neCessaries,
encouraged me to go beyond what I should have been able to have
done upon another occasion.

My raft was now strono' enough to bear any reasonable weight.
My next care was what toiload it with, and how to preserve what I
laid upon it from the surf of the sea; but I was not long considering
this. I first laid all the planks or boards upon it that I could get,
and having considered well what I most wanted, I got three of the
seamen's chests, which I had broken open and emptied, and lowered
them down upon my raft; these I filled with provisions, viz. bread,
rice, three Dutch cheeses, five pieces of dried oats' flesh (which we
lived much upon), and a little remainder of uuropean corn, which
had been laid by for some fowls which we had brought to sea. with
us, but the l'owls were killed. There had been some barley and

tion to see my cont, shirt, and waistcoat, which I had left. on shore,
upon the sand, swim away; as for my breaches, which were only
linen, and open-knead, I swam on board in them, and my stockings.
However, this put. me upon rummaging for clothes, of which 1 found

At that time. I got. it. down to my raft, even whole as it was,
without losing time to look into it, for I knew in general what. it
contained.

My next care was for some ammunition and arms. There were
two very good feuding-pieces in the real. cabin, and two pistols;

set. all my navigation. I hoped to find some creek or river which I
might make use of as a port. to get to land with my cargo.

opening of the land, and I found a strong current of the tide set

m-art; for, knowing not-hing,r of the coast, my raft ran aground at

As for the creature 1 killed, I took it. to be a kind of a hawk, its

I finished this work in about. a fortnight; and I think my powder,
which in all was about two hundred and forty pounds weight, was
divided into not less than a hundred pureels. As to the barrel that
had been wet, I did not apprehend any danger from that ; so I placed
it in my m-w cave, which, in my fancy, I called my kitchen, and the
rest I hid up and down in holes among the rocks, so that no wet
might come to it, marking vet" carefully where I laid it.

In the interval 01' time while this was doing, 1 went out at least.
once every day with my gun. One day I kitled a site-goat, which
had a little kid by her, which she gave suck to, which grieved tne
heartily; but. when the old one fell, the kid stood stock still by hcr,
till I came and took her up; attd not only so, but when I carried the
old one with me, upon my shoulders, the kid followed me quite to
my enclosure; upon which l laid down the dam, and took the kid
in my arms, and carried it over my pale, in hopes to have bred it up
lame; but it would not eat; so I was forced to kill it, and eat it
mySeIt'. These two supplied me with flesh a great while, for I eat
sparingly, and preserved my provisions (my bread especially) as
much as possibly 1 could.

And now being about to enter into a melancholy relation of a
scene of silent; life, such perhaps, as was never heard of in the world
before, I shall take it from its beginning, and continue it in its
order. It was, by my account, the 30th of September, when, in the
manner as above said, I first set foot upon this horrid island; when
the sun being to us in its autumnal equinox, was almost just over
my head : for I reckoned myself, by observation, to be in the latitude

of nine degrees twenty-two minutes north of the Line.

AFTER I had been there about ten or twelve days, it came into my
thoughts that I should lose my reckoning of time for want of books,
and pen and ink, and should even forget the Sabbath days from tho
working,r days: but, to prevent this, I cut it with my knife into a
large post, in capital letters; and making it into a great cross, I set
it. up on the shore where I first landed, viva, " l' ezunc on shore here
on the 30th of September, 1659." Upon the sides of this square
post. I out every day a notch with my knife, and every seventh notch
'as as long again as the rest, and every first day of the month as
long again as that long one: and thus I kept my calendar, or weekly,
monthly, and yearly reckoning of time.
But it happened, that among the many things which I brought out
of the ship, in the several my tires which, as above mentioned, I
made to it, I got several things it? less value, but not at all less
useful to me, which I found some time after, in rummaging thc chests :
as in particular, pens, ink, and paper; several parcels in the captain's,
mate's, gunner's, and carpenter's keeping; three or four compasses,
some mathematical instruments, dials, perspectives, charts, and books
of navigation; all of which I huddled together, Whether I might
want them or no: also I found three very trood Bibles, which came to
me in my cargo from Englandand which I had packed up among my
things; some Portuguese books also, and, among them, two or three
popish prayer-books, and several other books, all which 1 carefully
secured. And I must not forget, that we had in the ship a dog, and
two cats, of whose eminent histor I ma have occasion to say some-
thing, in its place; for I carried 0th t re cats with me; and as for
the dog he Jumped out of the ship himself, and swam on shore the
day after I went on shore with my first cargo, and was a trusty ser-
Vunt to me for many years: [wanted nothing that he could fetch
me, nor any company that he could make up to me, I only wanted
to have him talk to me, but that he could not do. As I observed
before, 1 found pens, ink, and paper, and l husbaudcd them to tnc
utmost; and I shall show that while my ink lasted,I kept things
very exact, but after that was gone I could not; for I could not
make any ink by any means that I could devise.

And this put me in mind that I wanted many things, notwithstand.
ing all that I had amassed together; and of these, this of ink was
one; as also a spade, pickaxe, and shovel, to dig or remove the
earth; needles, pins, and thread; as for linen, I soon learned to want
that without much dillicull. .

This want of tools mull: every work I did go on heavily: and it
was nearly a whole year before I had entirely finished my little pale.
or surrounded my habitation. The piles or stakes, which were as
heavy as 1 could well lift, were a long time in cutting and preparing
in the woods, and more by far in bringing home; so that I spent
sometimes two days in cutting and brinrrmg home one of those posts,
and a third in drivinv it into the ground; for which purpose, I got a
heavy peace of wood3 at first, but at last bethought me of one of the
iron crows; which, however,though I found it answer, made driving
these osts or piles very laborious and tedious work. But what
need {have been concerned at the tediousncss of an thing I had
to do, seeing. I had time enough to do it in; nor ha 1 any other
employment, if that had been over, at least that I could foresee,
except the ranging the island to seek for food; which I did more or
less, every day.

1 have already described my habitation, which was a tent under
the side of a rock, surrounded with a strong pale of posts and cables;
but I might. now rather call it a wall, for Iraised a kind of well
against it of turfs. about two feet thick on the omsidc: and after
some time (I think it was-a year and a half) I raised rafters from it,
leaning to the rock, and thatched or covered it with boughs of trees,
and such things as I could get, to keep out the rain; winch I found,
at some times of the year, very violent.

l have already observed how I brought all my goods into this pale,
and into the care which I had made behind me. But I must ob-
serve, too, that at first this was a confused heap of goods, which, as
they lay in no order, so they took up all my place; I had no room to
turn myself: so I set myself to enlarge my cave, and work further
into the earth; {or it was a loose sand rock, which yielded easily to
tho labour 1 bestowed upon it; am when I found I was prettv
sale as to the beasts of prey, I worked sideways, to the right hand,

 

into the rock, and then taming to the right again, worked nine
out, and made me a. door to conzc out in the outside of my pailc or
fortification.
This gave me not only egress and regress, as it were, a back way
to airy ten: and to my storehouse, but gave me room to stow my
0()( S.
g And now I began to apply myself to make such necessary things as
I found I most wanted, particularly a chair and a table; for without
those I was not able To enjoy the few comforts I had in the world;
I could not write, or eat, or do several things with so much pleasure;
without a table; so I wont to work. And here I must need ob-
serve, that as reason is the substance and original of mathematics,
so that by stating and squaring everything by reason, and by making
the most rational judgment of things, every man may he, in time,
master of every mechanic art. I had never handled a tool in my
life; and yet. in time, by labour, application, and contrivauce I found
at last, that I wanted nothing but I could have made, especially if I
had had tools. However, I made abundance of things, even without
tools; and some with no more tools than an adzc and a hatchet, which
erhaps were never made that way before, and ihat with infinite
lithour. For example, if I wanted a board, I had no other Way bnt
to cut down a tree, set it on an edge before me, and hew it flat on
either side with my axe, till 1 had brought it to be as thin as a plank,
and then dub it smooth with my adze. It is true, by this method, I
could make but one board of a whole tree; but this I had no remedy
for but patience, any more than I had for a prodigious deal of time
and labour which it took me up to make a plank or board: but my
time or labour was little worth, and so it was as well employed one
way as another.

I made me a table and a chair, in the first place; and this I did
out. of the short pieces of boards that I brought on my raft from the
ship. But when 1 wrought out some boards, as above,I made large
shelves, of the breadth of a foot and a half, one over another, all
along one side of my cave, to lay all my tools, nails and iron work
on; and, in a word, to separate every thing,r at large in their
places, that I might easily come at them. I knocked pieces into
the wall of the rock, to hang my guns, and all things that would
hang up; so that had my cave been seen, it looked like a general
magazine of all necessary things; and I had every thin so ready
at. my hand, that it was a great pleasure to me to see al my goods
in such order, and especially to find my stock of all necessaries so

rent.
g And now it was that I beoan to keep a journal of every day's em-
ployment; for, indeed, at lirst, I was in too much hurry, and not
only as to labour, but in much discomposure of mind; and my
journal would, too, have been full of many dull things.

Some days after this, and after I had heeii on board the ship and
got all that I could out of her, I could not forbcar getting up to the
top oEa little mountain, and looking out to sea, in hopes of seeing a
ship: then fancy that, at a vast distance, I spied a sail, please my-
self with the hopes of it, and, after looking steadily, till I was almost
blind, lose it quite, and sit down and weep like a child, and thus
increase my misery by my folly. "

But having got over these thin in some measure, and having
settled my household stud" and ha itation, made me a table and a
chair, and all about me as handsome as I could, I began to keep my
journal: of which I shall here give vou the copy (though init Will be
told all these particulars over again) as long wit lasted ; for having
no more ink, 1 was forced to leave it off.

I at first thought- these the pure productions of Providence for my

cease; and I must confess, my religious thankfulness to God's

was nothing but what>> was common; though I ought to have been
as thankful for so strange and unforeseen a providence, as if it had
been miraculous: for it was really the work of Providence, as to me,
that should order or a point that ten or twelve rains of corn should
remain nus oiled, when the rats had destro c all the rest, as if it
had been rcppcd from heaven; as also, hat I should throw it
cut- in that particular place, where, it being in the shade of a
high rock, it sprung up immediately; whereas, if I had thrown

destroyed.

I carefull saved the ears of this corn, you may be sure, in their

in its place.

APRIL 16. I finished the ladder; so I went u with the ladder
to the top, and then pulled it up after me, and et it down in the

The very next day after this wall was finished, I had almost all
my labour overthrown at once, and myself killed; the case was
thus ;---As I was busy in the inside of it, behind my tent, iust at the
entrance into my cave, I was terribly frightened by a shock of an
earthquake !

I was so much amazed with the thing itself (having never felt the
like, nor discoursed with any one that had) that I was like one dead
or stupiiied; and the motion of the earth made my stomach sick, like
one that was tossed at sea: but the noise of the falling of the rock
awakcd me, as it were, and rousing me from the stupified condition
I was in, filled me with horror, and I thought of nothing but the hill
falling upon my tent and my household goods, and burying all at
once; this sunk my Very soul within me a second time.

After the third shock was over, and I felt no more for some time,
I begin: to take couruoe; yet I had not heart enough to 0 over my
wall a vain, for fear of" being buried alive; but satst' upon the
groun greatly east down and disconsolatc, not knowmg what to do.
All this while I had not. the least. serious religious thought; nothing
but the common " Lord, llave mercy on me! " and when it was over
that went away too.

This set me thinking about what I had best do; conclud-
ing, that if the island was suh'eet to earthquakes, there would be
no living for me in a cave, but must consider of building me some
little hut in an open place, which I might surround with a wall, as I
had done here, and so make myself secure from wild beasts or men;
for if I staid where l was, I should certainly, one time or other, be
buried alive 9

3
With these. thoughts, I resolved to remove my tent from the place

MAY 1. In the morning, looking towards the seaside, the tide
being low, 1 saw something lie on the shore bigger than ordinary,
and it looked like a cask; when I came to it I found a small barrel,
and two or three pieces of the wreck of the ship, which were driven
on shore by the late hurricane; and looking towards the wreck itself,
I thought it seemed to lie higher out of the water than it used to do.
I examined the barrel that was driven on shore, and soon found it
was a barrel of gunpowder; but it had taken water, and the powder
was caked as hard as a stone; however, I rolled it further on the
shore for the present, and went on upon the sands, as near as I could
to the wreck of the ship, to look for more.

When I came down to the ship, I found it strangely removed.
The forecastie, which lay buried in the sand, was heaved up at least
six feet; and the stem (which was broke to pieces, and parted from
the rest, by the force of the sea, soon after I had left rummagino her)
was tossed, as it were, up, and east on one side: and the sun was
thrown so high on that side next her stem, that I could now walk
quite up to her when the tide was out.

This wholly diverted my the ' rrhts from the design of removing my
habitat-ion ; and I busied mysef mightily, that day especiallrv, in
searching whether I could make any way into the ship; ut I ound
nothing was to be expected of that kind, for all the inside of the ship
was choked u with sand. However, as I had learned not to (less
of withing, resolved to pull everything to pieces that I eoul of
the 5 ip, concluding that everything I could get from her would be

of some use or other to me.

I rounn now that the seasons of the year might generally be divided,

February, dry; the sun being then to the south of the Line.

The rainy seasons held sometimes longer and sometimes shorter,

I could not tell what part of the world this might be; otherwise
than that I knew it must be part of America, and as I concluded
by all my observations, must be near the Spanish dominions; and
perhaps was all inhabited by savages, where, if I should have landed,

had been in a worse condition than I was now. I therefore ac-
quiesced in the disposition of Providence, which I began now to
own and to believe ordered everything for the best ; I say, I quieted
in mind with this, and left oll' aillicting myself with fruitless wishes
0 being there. Besides, after some reflection upon this affair,
I considered that if this land was the Spanish coast, I should certainly
one time or other, see some vessel pass or rcpass one way or other;
but if not, then it was a savage coast between the Spanish country
and the Brazils, whose inhabitants are indeed the worst of savages;
for they are eannibals, or men-caters, and fail not to murder and
devour all human beings that fall into their hands.

With these considerations, walking very leisurely forward, I found
this side of the island, where I now was, much pleasanter than mine;
the open or savannah fields sweetly adorned with flowers and rass,
and full of very fine woods. I saw abundance of parrots; an fain
would have caught one, if possible, to have kept it to he tame, and
taught it to speak to me. I did, after taking some pains catch a
young parrot; for I knocked it down with a S'JCK, and, having recov-
ered it, I brought it home; but it was some years before I could
make him s ear; however, at last. I taught lnm to call me by my
name very amiliarly.

'l' 2
I was exceedingly amused with this journey. I found in the low
grounds hares, as I thought them to be, and foxes; but they differed
greatly from all the other kinds 1 had met with ; nor could I satisfy
myself to eat them, though I killed several. But I had no need to
be venturous; for I had no want of food, and of that which was very
good too , especially those three sorts, viz. goats, pigeons, and turtle,
or tortoxse.

.Is soon as I came to the sea-shore, I was surprised to see that I
had taken up my lot on the worst side of the island ; for here indeed
the shore was covered with innumerable turtles; whereas, on the
other side I had found but three in a year and a half. Here was
also an infinite number of fowls of many kinds ; some of which I had
not seen before, and many of them very good meat, but such as I knew
not the names of, except those culled penguins.

I confess this side of the country was much pleasant-er than mine;
yet I had not the least inclination to remove; for as l was fixed in my
tahitation, it became natural to me, and I seemed all the while I was
here to be as it were upon a journey, and from home. However, I
travelled along the seashore towards the east, Isuppose about twelve
miles, and then setting up a great pole upon the seashore for a mark,
Iconcluded I would go home again; and that the next journey I
took should be on the other side of the island, cast from my dwelling,
and so round till I came to my post again ; of which in its place.

I took another way to come back than that I went, thinking I could
easily keep so much of the island in my view, that I could not miss
finding my first dwelling by viewing the country ; but I found myself
mistaken; for beingr come about two or three miles, 1 found myself
desrended in a very large valley, but so surrounded with hills, and
those hills covered with wood, that I could not see which was my way
by any direction but that of the sun, nor even then, unless I knew
very well the position ofthe sun at that time of the day. And it
happened to my farther misfortune, that the weather proved hazy for
three or four days while I was in this valley; and not being able to
see the sun, I wandered about. verv uncomfortable, and at last was
obliged to find out the seaside, look for my post, and come back the
same way I went; and then by easy journeys I turned homeward,
the weather being exceeding hot, and my gun, ammunition, hatchet,
And other things very heavy.

It was now that I began sensibly to feel how much more happy the

and now having changed both my sorrows and my joys: my very

1n the midst of the greatest composurcs of my mind this would

but if I could burst- iuto tears, or give vent to my feelings by words,

'l'hus, and in this disposition of mind, I began my third year; and

abroad with my gun in the afternoon.

To this short time allowed for labour, l desire may be added the

My ('ase was this; it was a large tree that was to be cut down,

But as the beasts ruined me before, while my corn was in the

of them. I took them up, and served them as We serve notorious
thieves in England, viz. hanged them in chains, for a terror to others.
It is impossible to imagine that this should have such an effect as it
had; for the fools not only never came to the corn, but, in short,
they forsook all that part of the island, and I could never see a bird
near the place as ling as my scarecrou's hung there. This I

These rcilcelions made me very sensible of the goodness of Prop-

With these reflections I worked my mind up, not only to a resig-

stances, but even to a sincere thankfulness tor my condition; and
that I, who was yet a living man, ought not to complain, seeing

of wonders could have brought- ; that I ought to consider I had been

had no society, which was my aillietion on one hand, so I found no
ravenous beasts, no furious wolves or tigers, to threaten my life; no
venomous or poisonous creatures, which I might feed on to my hurt;
no savages to murder and devour me. In a word, as my life was a
life of sorrow one way, so it was a life of mercy another; and I
wanted nothing to make it a life of comfort, but. to make myself
seusrhlc of God's goodness to me, and care over me in this condition ;
and after 1 did make a just improvement of these things, I went
away, and was no more sad.

My clothes now began to decay mightily; as to linen. I had
none {or a great while, except some chequered shirts which 1
found in the chests of the other seamen, and which I carefully
preserved, because many times I could bear no clothes on but a
shirt, and it was a very great help to me that I had, among all
the men's clothes of the ship, almost three dozen of shirts.

I began to consider about putting the few rags I had, which I
called clothes, into some order. I had worn out. all the waistcoat:
I had. and my business was now to try if I could not make 'uckets

I have mentioned that I saved the skins of all the creatures that
I killed, I mean four-footed ones; and I had hung them up, stretched

useful. The first thing I made of these was a great cap for in

of the skins, that is to say, a waistcoat, and breaches open at the

than warm. I must not omit to acknowledwe that trey were
wretchedly made; for if I was a bad carpenter, was a. worse tailor
llowcver, they were such as Imado ver good shift with; and when

cap being uppermost, II was kept very dry.

After this, I spent a great deal of time and pains to make me an
umbrella. I made one and covered it with skins, the hair upwards,
so that. it cast off the ruin like a pent-house, and kept. of? the sun
so effectually, that. I could walk out in the hottest of the weather
with greater advantage than I could before in the coolest; and
when I had no need of it, could cluse it and carry it under my
arm.

Thus I lived mighty comfortably, my mind being entirely com-
posed by resigning to the will of God, and throwing myself
wholly upon the disposal of his roridenee. This made my life
better than sociable; for when began to regret the want of

It was on the sixth of November, in the sixth year of my reign, or
my captivity, which you please, that I set out on this voyage, and I
found it much longer than I expected ; for though the island itself
was not very large, yet when I came to the east side of it, I found a
great ledge of rocks lie out about two leagues into the so. , some
above water, some under it ; and beyond that a shoal of sand, lying
dry half a league more, so that I was obliged to go a great way out
to sea to double the point.

When first I discovered them, I was going to give over my enter-
prise, and come buck again, not knowing how far it might oblige mo
to go out to sea, and above all, doubting how I should get back
again; so I came to an anchor with a piece of a broken grappling
which I got out of the ship.

Having secured my boot, I took my gunned went on shore, climb-
ing up on u hill, which seemed to overlook that point, where I saw
the full extent of it, and resolved to venture.

In my \'it'willg the sea from that hill Where I stood, 1 crecived a
strong. and indeed a most furious current, which ran to t to east, and
even came close to the point; andI took the more notice of it,
because I saw there might be some. danger that, when I came into
it, I might be carried out to sea b' the stren lb of it, and not be
able to make the island again; an , indeed, bag I not get first upon
this hill, I believe it would have been so; for there was the some
current on the other side of the island, only that it set olf at a farther
distance, and I saw there was a strong eddy under the shore; so I
had nothing to do but to get out of the first current, and I should
presentlv be in an eddy.

I lay here. however, two days, because the wind blowing pretty
fresh at E. S. 15., and that being just contrary to the said current,
made n great breach of the sea upon the point; so that it was not
safe for me to keep too close to the shore, for the breach, nor to go
too far off, because of the stream.

The third day, in the morning, the wind having abated overnight,
the sea was calm. and I ventured; but I am a warningpiece again to
all rash and ignorant pilots; for no sooner was I come to the point,
when l was not even my boat's length from the shore, but I found
myself in a great depth of water, and a current like the sluice of a
null; it. carried my boat along with it with such violence, that all I
Could do could not keep her so much as on the (nine of it; but I
found it hurried me further and farther out from the eddy, which was
on my left hand. There was no wind stirring to help me, and all I
could do with my paddles signified nothing; and now I betran to give
mvself over for lost; for as the current was on both sides of the
island, I knew in a. few leagues' distance they must join again, and
then 1 was irrecorerably gone; nor did Isec any possibility of event-
ing it; so that I had no prospect before me but of perishing, not b
the sea, for that was calm enough, but of starving for hunger.

886 Anvzs'ruuns or uonmsou CRUSOB.

had indeed found a tortoise on the shore, as big almost as I could
lift, and had tessed it into the boat; and I had a great jar of fresh
water, that; is to say, one of my earthen pots; but. what was all this
to being driven into the vast ocean, where there was no shore, no
main land or island. for a thousand leagues at. least?

It is scarce possible to imagine the consternation I was now in,
being driven from my beloved island (for so it appeared to me now
to be} into the wide ocean, almost two leagues, and in the utmost.
despair of ever recovering it again. However, I worked hard. till
indeed my strength was almost exhausted, and lit'pl. my boat. as
nmch to the northward, that is, towards the side oi" the current
which the eddy lay on, as possibly I could; when about noon, as the
sun passed the meridian, I thought I felt. a little breeze of wind in
my taco, springing up from S.S.E. This cheered my heart a little,
and especially when, in about half an hour more, it blew a pretty
gentle gale. By this time I was got at a frightful distance from the
island. and had the. least cloudy or hazy weather intervened, I had
been undone another way too; for l had no compass on board, and
should never have. known how to have steered towards the island, if
1 had but once lost sight of it; but the weather continuing clear, 1
applied myself to get up my mast again, and spread my sail, stnnd~
ing away to the north as much as possible, to get. out of the
current.

Just as I had set my mast. and sail, and the boat began to stretch
away. 1 saw even by the clcarness ot' the water some alteration of
the current was near; for where the current was so strong, the
water was foul; but. perceiving the water clear, I found the current.
ahatc; and presently I found to the cast, at about. half a mile, a
breach of the sea upon some 1'"ka ; these rocks I found caused the
current to part again, and as the main stress of it. ran away more
southerly. leaving the rocks to the north-east, so the other returned
by the repulse ot the rocks, and made a st rung eddy, which mu back
again to the north-west, with a very sharp stream.

T hey who know what it is to have a reprieve brought. to them
upon the ladder, or to be rescued from thieves just. going to murder
them. or who have been in such-like extremities, may guess what
my present snr wise ol'joy was, and how gladly I put. my boat. into
the stream of t iis eddy; and the wind also freshening, how gladly I
spread mv sail to it, running cheerfully before the Wind, and with a
strong tide or eddy under toot.

'l'hts eddy carried me about a leagucin my way back again directly
towards the island, but about two leagues more to the northward
than the current which carried me away at first; so that when I
came near the island, I found myself open to the northern shore of
it, that is to say, the other end of the island, opposite to that which
I went out from.

When I had made something more than a league of way by the
help of this current or rddy, I found it was spent, and served me no
further. However, I found that being between two great currents,
viz. that on the south side, which had hurried me away, and that on
the north, which lay about a league on the other side; I say,
between these two, in the wake of the island, I found the water at
least, still, and running no way; and having still a breeze of wind
fair for me, I kept on steering directly for the island, though not
making such fresh way as I did before.

About. four o'clock in the evening, being then within a league of
the island, I found the point of the. rocks which occasioned this dis-
nster stretching out, as is described before, to the southward, and
casting oil' the current more southerly, had, of course, made another

 

eddy to the north; and this 1 found very strong, but not direct]
setting the way my course lay, which was due west, but almost fu
north. However, having a. fresh gale l stretched across this eddy,
slanting north-west; and, in about an hour, came within about a
in": of the shore, where, it being smooth water, I soon got to
an .

When I was on shore, I fell on my knees, and gave God thanks
for my deliverance, resolvin to lay aside all thoughts of my deliver.
ance by my boat ; and wires ling myself with such things as I had, I
brought my boat close to the shore, in a little cove that I had spied
under some trees, and laid me down to sleep, being quite spent with
the labour and fatigue of the voyage.
I now had enough of rambling to sea for some time, and had
enough to do for many days to >it still, and to reflect upon the
danger 1 had been in. I would have been ver glad to have had my
boat again on my side of the island; but I new not how it was
iraeIieuble to get it about. As to the east side of the island, which
i had gone round, I know well enough there was no venturin that
way ; my very heart would shrink, and In very blood run chi , but
to think of it; and as to the other side 0 the island, I did not know
how it might be there ; but supposing the current ran with the same
force against the shore at the east as it passed by it on the other, I
might run the same risk of being driven down the stream, and
carried by the island, as I had been before of being carried away
from it; so, with these thoughts, I contented myself to be without
any boat, though it had been the product of so many months' labour
to make it, and of so many more to get it into the sea.

in this government of my temper remained near a year, lived a
very sedate, retired life, as you may well suppose; and my thoughts
being very much composed, as to my condition, and fullv comforted
in resigning myself to the dispositions of Providence, I thought I
lived really very ha pily in all things, except that of society.

I improved myself in this time in all the mechanic exercises which
my necessities put me upon applyingmyself to ; and I believe I could,
upon occasion, have made a very good carpenter, especially consider-
ing how few tools I had.

Besides this, I arrived at an unexpected perfection in my earthen-
ware, and contrived well enough to make them with a wheel, which
I found infinitely easier and better; because I made things round
and sha cable, which before were filthy things indeed to look upon.
But I 1. link I was never more vain of my performance, or more joy'
fat for anything I found out, than for my being able to make a
tobacco-pipe: and though it was ave ugly clumsy thing when it
was done, and only burned red, like ot icr earthenware, yet it was
hard and firm, and would draw the smoke, I was exceedingly com-
forted with it, for I had always been used to smoke.

I began now to perceive my powder abated considerably ; this was
a want which it was impossible for me to 511 ply, and I began ser-
ionsl to consider what I must do when {should have no more
pow er, that is to say, how I should do to kill any goats. I had, an
is observed, in the third year of my being here, kept a. young kid,
and bred her up tame, and I was in hopes of ettin a lie-goat ; but
I could not by any means l)l'ln"' it to pass, tifi m gkid grew an old
mat; and as 1 could never find in my heart to ki her, she died at

11E BEARS A FLOCK OF GOATS--EIS DAIRY--HIS DOMESTIC HABITS
AND STYLE 0]." LIVING--INCREASING PROSPERITY.

BEING now in the eleventh year of my residence, and as I have said
my ammunition growing low, 1 set myself to study some art to trap
and snare the goats, to see whether 1 could not catch some of them
alive. I dug several large pits in the earth, in places where I had
observed the cats used to feed, and over those pits I placed hurdles,
of my own ma 'mg too, With a rent weight upon them; and several
times I put, ears of barlev and ry rice, without setting the trap; and
I could easily perceive that the gents had gone in and eaten up the
corn, for I could see the marks of their feet. At len [II I set three
traps in one night, and going the next morning, 1 ound them all
standing, and yet the bait eaten and gone. This was very discourab-
mg: however, 1 altered my traps; and one morning I found in one
otthem a large old he-goat, and in one of the others three kids, a
male and two temales.

As to the old one, I knew not what to do with him; he was so
fierce, l dursL not go into the it to him; that is to say, to go about
to bring him away alive, whic was what I wanted. I could have
killed him, but that was not my business, nor would it answer my
end; so I even let him out, and he ran away, as if he had been
frightened out. of his wits. I went to the three kids, and taking them
one b one, I tied them with strings together, and with some ditficulty

broug it them all home.
8'-
It was a good while before they would feed; hut throwing them
some sweet corn, it tempted them, and they begun to he tame. And
now I found that if I expected to supply myself with goats' flesh
when I had no powder or shot left, breeding some up tame was my
only way; when, erha is, I might have them about my house like a
flock of sheep. ut then it occurred to me, that I must keep the
tame from the wild. or else they would always run wild when the"
grew up; and the only way for this was, to have some enclosed piece
of ground, well fenced, either with hedge or pale, to keep them in so

 

gfi'octually, that those within might not break out, or those without
teak in.

But this was not all; for new I not only had goats' flesh to feed
on when I leased, but milk too; a thing which, indeed, in the he-
giuning. I id not so much as think of, and which, when it came into
my thoughts, was really an agreeable surprise; for now 1 set up my
dairy, and had sometimes a gallon or two of milk in a day. And as
Nature, who gives supplies of food to every creature, dictates even
naturally how to make use of it, so I, that had never milked a cow,
much less a goat, or seen butter or cheese made, only when l was a
hay, al'tera great many essays and miscarriages, made me both butter
and cheese at last, and also szdt (though I found it partly made to my
hand by the heat of the sun upon some of the rocks of the sea), and
never wanted it afterwards.
It would have made a stoic smile to have seen me and my little
family sit down to dinner. There was my ma'esty, the prince and
lord of the whole island; I had the lives of a 1 my subjects at my
absolute command; I could hang, draw, give liberty, and take it
away; and no rebels among all m subjects.

'I' tell to see how like a. kin!r I ined too, all alone, attended by my
servants; Poll, es if he had been my favourite, was the only mean
permitted to talk to me. My dog, who was now groom very o d and
crazy, and two cats, one on one side of the table, and one on the
ot her, expecting now and then a bit from my hand, as a mark of
special favour.

My own figure was uncouth enough. I had a great. high shapeless
cap, made of a. goat's skin, with a flap hanging down behind, as well
to keep the sun from me as to shoot the rain of? from running into
my neck ; nothin being so hurtful in these climates as the rain upon
the flesh, under t no clothes.

I had a short jacket of goat's skin, the skirts coming down to
about the middle of the thighs, and n pair of open-knead breeches of
the same; the breeches were. made of the skin of an old lie-goat,
whose hair hung down such a length on either side, that, like ante-
loons, it reached to the middle of my legs; stockings and s toes I
had none, but had made me a pair of somethings, I scarce know what
to call them, like buskins, to flap over my legs, and lace on either
side like spattcrdashes, but of a most barbarous shape, as indeed
were all the rest of my clothes.

I had on a broad belt of goat's skin dried, which I drew together
with two thongs of the same, instead of buckles; and in a kind 0!
fro" on either side of this, instead of a sword and dagger, hung a
little saw and a hatchet; one on one side and one on the other. I
had another belt, not so broad, and fastened in the some manner,
which hung over my shoulder; and at the end of it, under my left
arm, hung two pouches, both made of goat's skin too; in one or
which hung in powder, in the other my shot. At my hackI carried
my basket, an on my shoulder my gun; and over my head a great
clumsy ugly goat's skin umbrella, but which, after all, was the most
necessary thing I had about me, next. to my gun. As for my face,
the colour of it was not so mulatto-like as one might expect from a
man not at all careful of it, and living within nine or ten degrees ot
the equinox. My heard I had once sutl'crcd to grow till it was about
a narter of a yard long; but as I had both scissors and razor:
sut icient, I had cut it pretty short, except what grew on my upper lip,
which I had tr no cd into a large pair of Mahometan whiskers, such
as I had seen worn by some Turks at Sallee; for the Moors did not
wear such, though the Turks did; of these mustaehioa or whiskers,
I will not so they were long enough to hang my hat upon them, but.
they were 0 a length and shape monstrous enough, and such as, in
England, would have passed for frightful.
You are to understand, that now I had, as I may call it, two
plantations in the island; one my little fortification or tent with the
wall about it, under the rock, with the care behind me, which, by
this time, I had enlarged into several apartments or caves, one within
another. One of these, which was the driest and largest, and had a
door out beyond my wall or fortification, that is to say, bevoud
where my wall joined to the rock, was all filled up Will] the large
earthen pots, of which I have given an account, and with fourteen or

m han .

yAs for my wall, made, as before, with long stakes or piles, those
piles rew all like trees, and were by this time grown so big, and
sprea so very much, that there was not the least appearance, to
my one's view, of any habitation behind them.

Near this dwelling of mine, but a little further within the land,
and u )OII lower ground, lay mv two pieces of com land, which I
kept uly cultivated and sowed, and which duly yielded me their
harvest in its season; and whenever I had occasion for more com, I
had more land adjoining as fit as that.

Besides this, I had my country seat; and I had now a tolerable
plantation there also: for, first, I had my little bower, as I called it,
which I kept in repair; that is to say, I kept the. hedge which
encircled it. in constantly fitted up to its usual he: ht, the ladder
standing always in the inside: I kept the trees, whie at first were
no more than my stakes, but were now grown vcr firm and tall,
always out so, that they might. spread and grow thie ' and wild, and
make the more agreeable shade, which the did efi'eetuaily to my
mind. In the middle of this I had my tent. always standing, being a
piece of a sail spread over poles, set up for that purpose, and which
never wanted any repair or renewing; and under this I had made
me a squnb or cone 1, with the skins of the creatures I had killed,
and "'th other Soft things; and a blanket laid on them, such as he-
longed to our sea bedding, which I had saved, and a. great watch-
coat to cover me; and here, whenever I had occasion to be absent
from my chief seat, 1 took up my country habitation.

FIES HIS ABODB.

IT happened one day, about noon, going towards my boat, I was
CXCCCK ingly surprised with the print of a man's naked foot on the
shore, which was very plain to be seen in the sand. I stood like one
thunderstruck, or as if I had seen an apparition ; I listened, I looked
round me, but I could hear nothing, nor see anything; I went up to
a rising ground, to look further; I went up the shore and down the
shore, but it was all one; I could see no other impression but that
one. I went to it again to see if there were any more, and to
observe if it might not be my fancy; but there was no room for
that, for there was exactly the print. of a foot, toes, heel, and every
art of a foot: how it came thither I knew not, nor could I in the
east imagine; but, after innumerable fluttering thoughts. like a
man perfectly confused and out of myself. I came home to my forti-
fication, not feeling, as we say, the ground I went on. but terrified
to the last degree; looking behind me at every two or three Ste )8,
niistukin every bush and tree, and fancying every stump at a dis-
tance to c a man. Nor is it possible to describe how many various
shapes my alfrlghted imagination represented things to me in, how
many wild ideas were found every moment in my fancy, and what.
strange unaccountable whimsim came into my thoughts hy the way.
When I came. to my castle (for so I think I called it ever after
this). 1 fled into it like one normed; whether I went over by the
ladder, as first. contrived. or went in at lhe hole in the rock, which I
had called a door, I cannot i'cnimnher; no, nor could I renn'ndier
the next morning; for never frightened hare iled to cover, or fox to
earth with more terror of mind than I to this retreat.

l presently concluded that it. must be some of the. savages of the
main land over against me, who had wandered out. to son in their
cunoex and. eilhendriven by the currents or by contrary winds. had
made the island, and had liven on shore, but were gone away again
to sea; living as loth, perhaps. to have stayed in this dCSUluto island
as I would have been to have had them.

 

While these reflections were rolling upon my mind, I was very
thankful in my thoughts that. l was so happy as not to he thercabouis
at that lime, or tliat they did not see my boat, by which they would
have concluded that some inhabitants had been in the place, and
perhaps have searched further for me: then terrible thoughts racked
my imagination about their hnvintr l'ound my limit, and that there
were people here; and that if so, {should certainly have them come
again in greater numbers. and devour me: that if it should happen
so that they should not find me, yet they would find my enclosure,
destroy all my corn, and carry away all my flock of tame goats, and
1 should perish at last for mere wont.

'l'hus my fear banished all my religious hope, all that. former eon-
fidence in God, which was founded upon such wonderful experience
as I had had of His goodness, as if He that had fed me bv miracle
hitherto could not nrescrvc. by His power, the provision \ilhich lie
had made for me by His goodness. I repmaehed myself with my
laziness, that would not sow any more corn one year than would
just. serve me till the. next, season, as if no accident would intervene
to prevent my enjoying the crop that was upon the ground; and
this I thought so Just a reproof, that I resolved {or the. future to
have two or three year's corn beforehand. so that, whatever might
come, I might. not. perish for want of bread.

In the middle of these eogitations, apprehensions, and reflections,
it e:une into my thoughts one day, that all this might. be a mere
ehimera of my own, and that this font might be the print. of my
(out, when I came on shore from my beat; this cheered me up

 

a little too, and I began to persuade myself it was all a delusion,
that; it was nothing eise but my own foot: and why might I not.
come that way from the heat, as well as I was going that way to
the boat? Again, I consid "ed also, that I could by no means tell
for certain, where I had trail: and where 1 had not. : and that if, at
last, this was only the print of my own foot, 1 had played the part
of these fools, who try to make stories of spectres and apparitions,
and then are frightened at them more than anybody.

Now I began to take courage, and to loop abroad again, for I
had not stirred out of my castle for t-ltljt'c ays and nights, so taut. I
began to starve for provisions; for I had little or nothing within
d nu's but some barley cakes and water: then I knew that my goats
wanted to be milked too, which usually was my evening diversion;
and the poor creatures were in great pain and inconvenience for
want- of it: and, indeed, it almost spoiled some of them and almost
dried up their milk. Encouraging myself, therefore with the belief
that this was nothing but. the print. of one of my own feet,
and that 1 might be truly said to start at my own shadow, I beg:
to go abroad again, and went to my country house to milk my flock:
but. to see with what fear I went forward, how often I looked be-
hind me, how 1 was ready. every now and then, to lay down my
basket, and run for my life, it would have made any one think I
was haunted with an evil conscience, or that I had been latel most
terribly frightened; and so, indeed, I had. However, as went
down thus two or three days, and having seen nothing, I began to
be a little bolder, and to think there was really nothing:r in it but,
am own imagination; but I could not persuade myself folly of this

none to the place, first, it appeared evidently to me, that. when I

Thus I took all the measures human prudence could suggest for

While this was doing, 1 was not altogether careless of my other

he sullicicnt for me, without the expense of powder and shot, but

over again.

For this purpose. after long consideration, I could think of but
two ways to preserve them; one was, to find another convenient

 

place to dig a care under ground, and to drive them into it eve
night; and the other was, to enclose two or three little bits of lau ,
remote from one another, and as much concealed as 1 could, where
I might keep about half a dozen young goats in each place ; so that
if any disaster happened to the flock in general, I might be able to
raise them again with little trouhle and time; and this, though it
would require a great deal of time and labour, I thought was the
most rational design.

IN this disposition I continued for near a year after this. I kept
myself more retired than over, and seldom went from my cell, except
upon my constant employment. viz. to milk my she-goats, and man-'
age my little flock in the wood, which, as it was quite on the other

made me cautions, as well as before.

I believe the reader of this will not think it strange if I confess
that those anxieties, these constant dangers I lived m, and the con-
cern that was now upon me, put an end to all invention, and to all
the contrivanecs that I had laid for my future accommodations and
conveniences. I had the care of my safety more now upon my hands
than that of my food. I cared not. to drive a nail, or chop a stick
of wood new, for fear the noise I might make should be heard;
much less would I fire a. gun, for the same reason: and, above all, I
was intolei-ahly uneasy at making any lire, lest the smoke, which E:
visible at a great distance in the day, should betray me. For this
reason, I removed that part of my business which required fire,
such as burning of pets, iies, &c. into my new apartment in the
woods; where. after I has can some time, I found. to my unspeek~104 anvmrentzs or nomnsott caosoz.

able consolation, a. mere natural cave in the earth, which went in a

my man else, but one who, like me, want-ed nothing so much as a
sale retreat.

I was now recovered from my surprise, and begtut to look round

The next day after I came home to my hutch with him, I began

which, if it had been attenuated on the outside, would not have
opened at all, but would have fallen down, and made a great noise:
as to weapons, I took them all into my side every night. But I
needed none of all this precaution; for never man had a more faith-
ful, loving, sincere servant than Frida was to me; without pas-
sions. sullenness, or designs, perfectly ohliged and engaged--his very
affections were tied to me, like those of a child to a father; and I
dare say, he would have sacrificed his life for the saving mine upon
any occasion whatsoever: the many testimonies he gave me of this
put it out of doubt, and soon convinced me that I needed to use no
precautions as to my safety on his account.

This frequently gave me occasion to observe, and that with
wonder, that however it had pleased God. in his providence,
and in the government of the works of his hands, to take from
so great a. part of the world of his creatures the best uses to which
their faculties and the powers of their souls are adapted, yet that he
has bestowed upon them the same powers, the some reason, the
same affections, the same sentiments of kindness and obligation, the
same passions and resentments of wrongs, the some sence of grati~
tude, sincerity, iihelity, and all the capacities of doing good, and
reccuving good, that he has given to us; and that when he pleases
to offer them occasions of exerting these they are as ready, nay, more
ready, to apply them to the right uses for which they were bestowed,
than we are.

With my new companion I was reatly delighted, and made it my
business to teach him everything t rat was proper to make him use-
ful, handy, and helpful; at especially to make him speak, and
understand me when I spoke: and he was the nptest sciolar that

stand him, that it was very pleasant to me to talk to him. Now
my life began to be so easy, that I began to say to myself, that
could I have been safe from more savages, I cared not if I was
never to remove from the place where I lived.

By this I understood that my man Friday had formerly been among
the savages who used to come on shore on the farther part of the
island, on the same man-eating occasions he was now brought. for:
and sotne time after, when I took the courage to carry him to that
