place, and told me he was there once when they ate up twenty men,
two women, and one child ~ he could not tell twenty in English, but
he numbered them, by laying so many stones in a row, and pointing
to mo to tell them over,

I have told this passage, because it introduces what follows; that
after I had this discourse with him, I asked him how far it was from
our island to the shore, and whether the canoes were not often lost.
He told me there was no danger, no canoes ever lost; but that, after
a little way out to sea, there was a current and wind, always one
wnv in the morning, the other in the afternoon. This I understood
to he no more than the sets of the tide, as going out or coming in;
but I afterwards understood it was occasioned by the great draft
and reflux of the mighty river Oroonoko, in the mouth or gulf of
which river, as I found afterwards, our island lay; and that this
land which I erccircd to the W. and NW. was the great island
Trinidad, on t e north point of the mouth of the river. I asked
Friday a thousand questions about the country, the inhabitants, the
sea, the coast, and what nations were near: he told me all he knew,
with the greatest openness imaginable. I asked him the names of
the several nations of his sort of pcople, but could get no other
name than Caribs: from whence I easily understood, that these were
Curibbees, which our maps place on the part of America which
reaches from the mouth of the river Oroonoko to Guiana, and on-
wards to St. Martha. He told me that up a great we beyond the
moon, that was, beyond the setting of the moon, w ich must be
west from their country, there dwelt white bearded men, like me,
and pointed to my great whiskers, which I mentioned before; and
that they had killed much mans, that was his word: by all which I
understood, he meant the Spaniards, whose cruelties in America had
been spread over the whole country, and were remembered by all
the nations, from father to son.

I inquired if he could tell me how I might go from this island and
get among these white men; he told me: "Yes, yes, you may go in
two Canoe." I could not. understand what he meant, or make him
describe to me what he meant by two cnnoc; till, at last, with great
diillculty, I found he meant it. must. be iii-n large boat, as big as two
canoes. This part of Friday's discourse begun to relish with me
very well: and from this time 1 entertained some hopes that, one
time or other, I might liud an opportunity to make my escaie from
this place, and that this poor sarnge might. be a means to he p me.

During the long, time that Friday had now been with me, and that
he began to spcsz to me, and understand me, I was not wantin to
lay a foundation of religious knowledge in his mind ; particular y I
asked him, one time, who made him? The poor creature did not
understand me at all, but thought I had asked him who was his
father; but I took it up by another handle, and asked him who
made the sea, the ground we walked on, and the hills and woods?
He told me, it was one old Benamuckee, that lived beyond all; he
could describe nothing of this great person, but that he was very
old, much older, be said, than the sea or the land, than the moon or
the stars. I asked him then, if this old person had made all things
why did not all thinws worship him? He looked very grave, en

with a crfect look of innooence, said: " All things say to him."
I asked him "if the eople who die in his country went own any

where?" He said, " es: they all went to Benamnekee;" t en I
ashgd gm :' whether these they ate up went thither too?" He
sat , " es.'

From these thin s I began to instruct him in the knowledfie of
the true God: I to d him that the great Maker of all things 'ved .
up there, pointing up towards heaven; that he governed the world

K

 

by the same power and providence by which he made it; and that
he was omnipotent, and could do ever thing for us, give everything
to us, take everything from us; and t nus, by degrees, I opened his
8 es. He listened with great attention, and received with leasure
t e notion of Jesus Christ being sent to redeem us, an of' the
manner of making our prayers to God, and his being able to hear us,
even in heaven. He told me one day, that if our God could hear us
up beyond the sun, he must needs be a greater God than their Ben-
emuckec, who lived but a little way off, and yet could not hear till
they went up to the great mountains where he dwelt to speak to
him. I asked him if ever he went thither to speak to him? He
said: "No ;" they never went that were young men; none went
thither but the old men, whom he called their Oowookakee: that is,
as I made him explain to me, their religious, or elerg ; and that
they went to say 0 (so he called saying prayers), an then came
back, and told them what Benamuckee said.

I
I endeavoured to clear 11' this fraud to my man Friday; and told
him, that the pretence of treir old men gorng up to the mountains
to say 0 to their god Benamuckce was a cheat: and that their
bringrng word from thence what he saidwns much more so; that if
they met with any answer, or spake with any one there, it must be
an evil spirit; and then I entered into along discourse about the
devil, the original of him, his rebellion against God, his enmity to
man, the reason of it, his setting himself up in the dark parts of the
world to be worshipped instead of God, and as God, and the many
str'utagems he made use of to deludc mankind to their ruin; how he
had a secret access to our passions and to our affections, and to
adapt his snares to our inclinations, so as to cause us even to be our
own tempters, and run upon our destruction by our own choice.

I had, God knows, more sincerity than knowledge in all the
methods I look for this poor creature's instruction, and must
acknowledge, what I believe all that not upon the same principle
will find, that in laying things open to him, I rcallv informed and
instructed myself in runny things that cit her 1 did not Know, or and
not fully considered before, but which occurred naturally to my
mind upon searching into them, for the infornmtion of this poor
savage: and 1 had more ufi'eetion in my inquiry utter things upon
this occasion than ever i felt before: so that, wlletlrcl' this poor wild
wreteh was the better for me or no, I had great reason to be thank-
ful that ever he came to me; my rief sat lighter upon me; :3
habitation grew comfortable tome icyond measure; and when
reflected, that in this solitary life vlrich I had been confined to, I
had not only been moved to look up to Heaven myself, and to seek
to the hand that had lu'oucht me here, but was now to be made an
instrument, under Providence, to save the life, and, for ought I
knew, the soul, of a poor savage, and bring him to the true know-
h-dge of religion, and of the Christian doctrine, that he might know
Christ Jesus, in whom is life eternal; I say, when I reflected upon
all those things, a secret joy ran through every part of m soul, and
I frequently rejoiced that ever I was brought to this pace, which
] lr:rd south-n thought the ruest dreadful of all alllietions that could
possibly have lie-fallen me.

SECTION XXVIII.

ROBINSON GOES TO LISBON, WHERE HE FINDS THE PORTUGUES,
CAPTAIN, "'IIO BENIN-"LS "I." AN ACCOUNT OF HIS PROPERTY IN
THE Blt;\ZlLS.--SE1'S OUT ON HIS RETURN TO ENGIAND BY LAND.

Wnnn I came to England, I was as perfect a. stranger to all the
world as it' I had never been known there. My benefactor and
faithful steward, whom I left my money in trust with, was alive, but
had had great misfortunes in the world; was become a widow the
second time, and very low in the world. I made her very easy as to
what she owed me, assuring her I would give her no trouble; but on
the contrary, in rutitudc tor former care and faithfulness to me, I
relieved her as "'51 us my little stock would ufl'ord; which, at the time,
would allow me to do but little for her; but I assured her I would
never forget. her former kindness to me; nor did I forget her when
I had suttieient to help her, as shall he observed in its proper place.
I went down afterwards into Yorkshire: but. my father and mother
were dead, and all the family extinct, except that I found two sisters
and two of the children of one of my brothers, and as I had been
long ago given over for dead, there had been no provision made for
me : so that. in a. word, 1 found nothin" to relieve or assist me: and
that the little money I hud would not do much for me as to settling
in the world.

I met. with one piece of gratitude, indeed, which I did not: expect;
nnd this was, that the master of the ship whom I so happily
delivered, and by the same menns saved the ship and cargo, havin
given a very handsome account to the owners of the manner how
had saved the lives of the men and the ship, they invited me to meet
them, and some other merchants concerned, and all together made

We were not gone half over the plain, when we began to hear the
wolves howl in the wood on our left in a frightful manner, and
presently after, we saw about a hundred coming on directly towards
us, all in a body, am! most of them in a line, as regularly as an army
drawn up by an experienced ofliccr. I scarce knew in what manner
to receive them, but found to draw ourselves in a close line was the
only way ; so we formed in a. moment: but that we might not have
too much interval, I ordered that only over other man should
tire, and that the others who had not tired shoe (1 stand ready to give

us; and then that tiose who ha fired at first should not pretend to
loud their fuses again, but stand ready every one with a pistol, for
we were all armed with a fuse and a pair of pistols etch man; so we
Were, by this method, able to fire six volleys, hall' of us at a limo,
llowerer, at present we had no necessity, for upon firing the first
volley, the enemy made a full stop, being terrified as well with the
noise as with the. live; four of them. being shot. in the head, dro I ed;
several others were wounded, and went bleeding all, us we could, see
by the snow. I found they stop )cd. but did not immediately retreat;
whereupon, remembering that had been told that the fiercest crea-
tures were terrified by the voice. of a luau, I caused all the company
to halloo as loud as we could: and I found the notion not. altogether
mistaken; for upon our shout, they began to retire and turn about.
l then ordered a second volley to be tired in their rear, which put
them to the gallop, and away they went to the woods. 'I'his gave us
leisure to charge our pieees again -, and that we might lose no time,
we kept. going; we had but little more than loaded our fuses, and
put. ourselves in It' tiness. when we heard a terrible noise in the
same wood, on our left, only that it was farther onward, the same
way we were. to go.

The night. was coming on. and the. light began to be dusky, which
made it worse on our side; but the noise increasing. we, could easily
perceive that, it was the howling-and yelling of these hellish creatures;
and on a sudden we perceived twr~ or three troops of wolves, one on
our left, one behind us. and one in our front, so that. we seemed to
be surrounded with them, however, as they did not. fall upon us, we
kept. our way forward. as fast. as we could make our horses go, which,

were to ass, at. the farther side of the plain; but u e were greatly

iappened very much to our advantage, that. at the entrance into the

plaeing ourselves in a line behind one long tree, I advised them all

so, and it was well we. did; for never was a more furious charge
titan the creatures made upon us in this place. They came on will;
a growling kind of noise, and mounted the piece of timber, which, as
1 said, was our breastworlr, as if they were only rushing upon their
prey; and this fury of theirs, it seems, was principally occasioned by

fore, every other man; and they took their aim so sure, that they
killed several of the wolves at the first volley; but there was a
iii-"essity to keep a continual firing, for they came on like devils,
those behind pushing on those before.

\-\'hen we had fired a second Volley of our fuses, we thought they
stopped a little; and I hoped they would have gone off ; but it was
on: a moment, for others came forward again; so we fired two
vol eys of our pistols; and I believe in these four firings we had

crying and bowling they made was better understood by their fellows;

AIKIUVIEN A'l' ms ISLAND.

Tm: wind continued fresh ensterly, but. the weather otty good, and
as the winds had continued in the points between gilt}. and 8.19. a
long time, we missed several opportunities of sending them to
France; for we. met several ships hound to Europe, whereof two
were From-h, from St. Christopher's; but they had heen so long
heating up against the wind, that they durst tnke in no passen era,
for fear of wanting provisions for the voyage, as well for t cm-
selves us for those. that the should take in ; so we. were obliged to
0 on. It. was :lllOllL a weed after this that. we made the Banks of
ewfonndiand: where, to shorten my story, we put all our French
people on honrd a harque, whit-h they hired at sea there. to put them
on shore, mul aftm-wmds to carry them to France. if they could get
provisions to viotnnl tln-mselves with. When 1 say all the French
went on shore. I should remember that a yonnw priest on board,
henring we wore bound to the East Indies, desired to go the vo age
will! us, and to he. set on shore on the coast of Cormnandcl; w ich
l readily agreed to, for l wonderfully liked the man, and had very
good reason, as will appear afterwards: also four of the seamen
entered themselves on our ship. and proved very useful fellows.
From hence we directed our eonrsc to the West Indies, steering
away 8. end S. by E. for about. twenty days together, sometimes
little or no wind at all ; when we met with another subject. for our
humanity to work upon, almost us deplorable as that before.
It mm in the latitude of twentyoeven degrees five minutes north,
on the 19th day of March, 1694-5, when we spied a sail, our course
1'! 0194 onemunes or noatxsoa meson.

S E. and by S.: we soon perceived it was a large vessel. and that
she bore up to us, but could not at. first know what to make of her,
till, after coming a little nearer, we found that she had lost her main
topmasl, foremast, and bowsprit; and presently she fired a gun, as
a signal of distress: the weather was retty 0d, wind at N.N.W.,
a fresh gate, and we soon came to 3 on with er.

We found her a shi) of Bristo, bound home from Barbadoes,
but had been been bown out of the road at Barbadoes a few
days before she was ready to sail, by a terrible hurricane, while
the captain and chief-mate were both gone on shore; so that, besides
the terror of the storm, they were in an indifferent case for good
artists to bringr the ship home. They had been already nine weeks
at. sea, and had met with another terrible storm, after the hurricane
was over, which had blown them quite out of their knowledge to
the westward, and in which they lost. their masts, as above. they
told us they expected to have seen the Bahama islands, but were
then driven away again to the south-east, by a strong gale of wind
at N..'\'_\V., the same that blew now: and having no sails to work
the ship with but a mainnoursc, and a kind of square sail upon a
jury foremost, uhirb they had set at, they could not. lie near the
wind, but were omleavonriug to stan away for the Canaries.

But that which was worst of all was, that they were almost starved
for want of provisions, besides the fatigues they had undergone:
their bread and flesh were quite gone; they had not one ounce left
in the ship, and had none left for eleven days. The on] relief they
had was, their water was not all spent, and they had at out half a
barrel of flour left; they had sugar enough; some snceades, or
swcctmeats, they had at first, but they were devoured; and they
had seven casks of rum.

There were a youth and his mother, and a maid-servant, on board,
who were going; passengers, and thinking the ship was ready to sail,
unhappily came on board the evening before the hurricane began;
and having no provisions of their own left, they were in a more dc-
plorable condition than the rest: for the seamen, being reduced to
such an extreme necessity themselves, had no compassion, we may
be sure, for the poor passengers: and they were, indeed, in a con-
dition, that their misery is Very hard to describe.

I had perhaps not known this wart, if my enriosity had not led
me (the weather beinrY fair, and tie wind abated) to go on board
the ship. The second ,mate, who, upon this occasion, commanded
the ship, had been on board our ship, and he told am, indeed, they
had three passengers in the great cabin, that were in a deplorable
condition: "Nay," says he, "I believe they are dead, for 1 have
heard nothing of them for above two davs; and I was afraid to
in are after them," said he, "for I had nothing to relieve them with."

'Ve immediately applied ourselves to give them what relief we
could spare- and, indeed, I had so far overruled things with my
nephew, that I would have victuslled them, though we had
away to Virginia, or any other part of the coast of America, to ave
supplied ourselves; but there was no necessity for that.

But now they were inanew danger ; for they were afraid of
eating too much, even of that httle we gave them. The mute or
commander brought six men with him in his boat; but. these poor
wretches looked liked skeletons, and were so weak, that they could
hardly sit to their oars. The mate himself was very ill, and hall'--
starved, for he declared he had reserved nothing from the men, and
went share and share alike with them in every bit they ate.

1 cautioned him to eat sparingly, but set meat beiore him imme-
diately; and he had not eat three mouthfuls before he began to be
sick, and out of order; so he stopped awhile, and our sur eon mixed
him up smnethin with some brot , which he said woul he to him
both lood and piysic; and after he had taken it, he grew better.
In the meantime, I forgot not the men; I ordered victuals to be
given them; and the poor creatures rather devoured than ate it:
they were so exceeding hungry, that they were in a kind ravenous,
and had no command of themselves; and two of them ate with so
mueh'grcediness, that they were in danger of their lives the next
morning.

While I kept the mate, whom we then called captain, on board
with his men, to refresh them, so I also forgot not the starving crew
that. were left on board; but ordered my own boat to go on board
the ship, and, with my mate and twelve men. to carry them a sack
of bread, and four or live pieces of beef Io hoil. Our surgeon
charged the men to cause the meat to be boiled while they stayed,
and to keep guard in the cook-room to prevent the men takin it to
eat raw, or taking it out of the pot before it was well boils. , and
then to give every man but a very little at a time: and l) this
caution he preserved the men, who would otherwise have riled
themselves with that very food that was given them on purpose to
save their lives.

At the same time, I ordered the mate to go into the great cabin,
and see what condition the poor passen is were in; and if they
were alive, to comfort them, and give tiem what refreshment was
proper: and the our on gave him a large pitcher, with some of the

This deliverance tamed our Englishmen for a great while; the

It was some while before any of them could be taken; but being

The poor wreiehes, thoroughly humbled, and reduced in number
to about thirty-seven, closed with the preposal at the first offer, and
begged to have some food given them; upon which, twelve Spaniards
on "we Englishmen, well armed, with three Indian slaves and old
Friday, marched to the place where they were. The three Indian
slaves carried them a large quantity of bread, some rice boiled up to
cakes and dried in the sun, and three live goats; and they were
ordered to go to the side of a hill, where they sat down, ate their
provisions very thankfully, and were the most faithful fellows to
their words that could be thought of; for, except when they came
to be victunls and directions, they never came out of their bounds:
8111M t ere they lived when I came to the island, and I went to see
t em.

They had taught them both to plant corn, make bread, breed tame
goats, and milk them; they wanted nothing but wives, and they
soon would have been a nation. They were confined to a neck of
land, surrounded with high rocks behind them, and lying lain
towards the sea. before them, on the southeast corner of the isiimd.
They had land enough, and it was very good and fruitful; about
a mile and a half bread, and three or four miles in length.

Our men taught them to make wooden spades, such as I made for
myself, and gave among them twelve hatchets and three or four
klliVCs; and there they lived, the most subjected, innocent creatures
that ever were heard of.

After this, the colony enjoyed a perfect tran uillity with respect
to the savages, till [ came to revisit them, w 'ch was about two
years after; not but that, now and then, some canoes of savages
Came on shore for their triumihal, unnatural feasts; but as they
were of several nations, and per ups had never heard of those that
came before, or the reason of it, they did not make any search or
inquiry after their countrymen; and if they had, it would have been
Very hard to have found them out.

Thus, I think, 1 have given a full account of all that happened to
them till my return, at least, that was worth notice. The Indiana
or savages were wonderfully civilized by them, and they frequently
went among them; but forbade, on pain of death, any one of the
Indians coming to them. because they would not have their settle-
ment betrayed again. One thin was very remarkable, viz. that they
tau rht the savages to make wic er-work, or baskets, but they seen
out id their masters; for they made abundance of most ingenious
things in wicker-work, particularly all sorts of baskets, sieves, bird-
e "es, cupboards, &c.; as also chairs to sit on, stools, beds, couches,
mi abundance of other thin . being very ingenious at such work,

when they were once put int c way of it.

II M'ING thus given an account of the colony in general. and pretty
much of my live runagnte Englishmen, I must. say something of the
Spaniards, who were the main body of the family, and in whose
story there are some incidents also remarkable enough.

1 had a great. many discourses with them about their circumstances
when they were among the savages. They told me readily that they
had no instances to give of their application or ingenuity in that.
Country; that they were a poor, miserable, and dejected handful of
people; that. if menus had been put into their hands, yet they had so
nbzunloned themselves to despair, and so sunk under the weight of
their misfortunes, that they thou ht of nothing but. starving. One
of them, a grave sensible man, to d me he was convinced they were,
in the wrong; that it was not the port of wise men to give them-
selves up to their misery, but. always to take hold of the helps which
reason otl'ered, as well for present support as for future deliverance:
he told me that grief was the most senseless insignificant passion in
the world, for that it re arded only things past, which were gene-
rally impossible to be reezfiled, or to be remedied, but had no views
of things to come, and had no share. in anything that looked like
deliverance, but rather added to the ntlhct ion than proposed a
remedy ; and upon this be. repeated 11 Spanish proverb, which though
I cannot repeat in just the same words that he spoke it in, yctl
remember I made it In") an English proverb of my own thus :

In trouble to be troubled,
Is to have your trouble doubled.

[lo-ran on then in remarks upon all the little improvements I had
made- in my solitude; mv unwenried application, as he called it; and
how I had made a condition which in Its circumstances was at first
much worse than theirs, a thousand times more happy than their:
was even now when they were all together. He told me it was
remarkable that. Englishmen had a greater presence of mind, in their
distress, than on people he ever met With; that their unhappy
nation and the ortuguesc were the worst men in the world to
struggle with misfortunes; for that their first step in dun ('J'S, after

I was now, as near as I can compute, in the heart of China, about;

We were twenty-five davs travelling to Pckin, through a country

called.
